  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

<span id="freeTextContainer2193511217755804631">'My father died eleven years ago. I was only four then. I never thought I'd hear from him again, but now we're writing a book together'<br/><br/>To Georg Røed, his father is no more than a shadow, a distant memory. But then one day his grandmother discovers some pages stuffed into the lining of an old red pushchair. The pages are a letter to Georg, written just before his father die</span>
  <span id="freeText2193511217755804631" style="display:none">'My father died eleven years ago. I was only four then. I never thought I'd hear from him again, but now we're writing a book together'<br /><br />To Georg Røed, his father is no more than a shadow, a distant memory. But then one day his grandmother discovers some pages stuffed into the lining of an old red pushchair. The pages are a letter to Georg, written just before his father died, and a story, 'The Orange Girl'. <br /><br />But 'The Orange Girl' is no ordinary story - it is a riddle from the past and centres around an incident in his father's youth. One day he boarded a tram and was captivated by a beautiful girl standing in the aisle, clutching a huge paper bag of luscious-looking oranges. Suddenly the tram gave a jolt and he stumbled forward, sending the oranges flying in all directions. The girl simply hopped off the tram leaving Georg's father with arms full of oranges. Now, from beyond the grave, he is asking his son to help him finally solve the puzzle of her identity.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2193511217755804631" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/243097-what-do-you-think-if-we-make-a-story-love">What do you think if we make a story love with a lot of adventures like this book and then write it in a paper for our daughters and sons in the future?</a>
</div>

<ul class='questionFooterActions'>
<li class='footerItem like'>

<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/29756603-krisz"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1442983153i/29756603._UX100_CR0,25,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/29756603-krisz">Krisz</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">Our daughters and sons DO know how we met - and there are so many more things that are much more interesting and worthy to tell them than the meeting <a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">Our daughters and sons DO know how we met - and there are so many more things that are much more interesting and worthy to tell them than the meeting of their parents. Sure it's fun, but nothing more. Any little story about THEM as kids beats such in any given moment.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>

<span id="freeTextContainer9296237823423465759">Appelsinpiken = The Orange Girl, Jostein Gaarder<br/><br/>The Orange Girl is a Norwegian movie released in February 2009. It is based on a book by author Jostein Gaarder; a book translated to 43 languages. <br/><br/>Georg Røed's father Jan Olav died when he was four years old.   <span id="freeText9296237823423465759" style="display:none">Appelsinpiken = The Orange Girl, Jostein Gaarder<br /><br />The Orange Girl is a Norwegian movie released in February 2009. It is based on a book by author Jostein Gaarder; a book translated to 43 languages. <br /><br />Georg Røed's father Jan Olav died when he was four years old. Eleven years later, Georg's grandmother finds letters addressed to Georg from Jan Olav, written before his death, along with a story titled "The Orange Girl."<br /><br />As Georg soon discovers, "The Orange Girl" is not simply a story, but a riddle from the past that centers around an incident from his father's youth. In the story, it is revealed that Jan Olav had once boarded a tram and had taken notice of a beautiful girl who had been clutching a bag of oranges. When tram abruptly jolted, the girl's oranges were dispersed. Jan Olav attempted to collect the oranges, but found that the girl had already disembarked from the tram. The story is a request for his son to solve the mystery of the orange girl's identity.<br /><br />تاریخ نخستین خوانش: ماه جولای سال 2008 میلادی<br /><br />عنوان: دختر پرتقالی؛ نویسنده: یوستاین (یوستین) گاردر (گوردر)؛ مترجم: بابک تبرایی؛ مشخصات نشر: تهران، نیلا، 1385، در 144ص، شابک 9648573581؛ موضوع داستانهای نویسندگان نروژی سده 20م<br /><br />عنوان: دختر پرتقالی؛ نویسنده: یوستاین (یوستین) گاردر (گوردر)؛ مترجم: مهوش خرمی پور؛ ویراستار ویدا اسلامیه؛ مشخصات نشر تهران، تندیس، چاپ اول 1385، چاپ پنجم 1388، در 188ص، شابک 9789648944136؛<br /><br />پدر «جرج» در چهار سالگی او فوت کرده، اکنون «جرج» پانزده ساله است، و یازده سال پس از مرگ پدرش، نامه ای از ایشان پیدا میکند، که خطاب به «جرج» نوشته شده است؛ پدرش که گویا از درگذشت نزدیک خویش آگاه بوده، طی نامه ای، شرح آشنایی و عشق خویش به یک دختر پرتقالی (منظور میوه پرتقال است) را بیان میکند؛ میگوید: من ناراحتم که به دنیا آمدم تا وسط خوشیهایم از دنیا بروم؛ و از «جرج» میپرسد («جرج» اگه تو موقع تشکیل جهان بهت میگفتن وقتی زمانش برسه میتونی بری دنیا را ببینی اما وقتی زمانش رسید باید بیای بیرون ازش هر وقت که میخواد باشه آیا دلت میخواست اصلا به دنیا بیای؟)؛ کتاب این پرسش را طرح میکند «اگر حق انتخاب با شما بود آیا به دنیا میآمدید؟»؛<br /><br />تاریخ بهنگام رسانی 10/06/1399هجری خورشیدی؛ ا. شربیانی</span>
  <a data-text-id="9296237823423465759" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_980541512" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/980541512" />
    <a title="Kaya" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15462098-kaya"><img alt="Kaya" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1485382914p2/15462098.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/980541512?book_show_action=true">Jun 28, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kaya" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kaya" href="/user/show/15462098-kaya">Kaya</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15462098-kaya?shelf=5-stars">5-stars</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer980541512" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13300017505237209324"><b> <i>“My father died eleven years ago. I was only four then. I never thought I’d hear from him again, but now we’re writing a book together.”</i> </b><br/><br/>Sometimes, there's a disarming beauty in simplicity. Sometimes, describing ordinary feelings and doubts is enough for writing extraordinary books. This is the case. I'm so happy that this is my first Gaarder's book. I love how he evokes emotions in the reader's mind without even trying to manipulate them. <br/><br/>The plot isn't quite about philosophy, but it also isn'</span>
  <span id="freeText13300017505237209324" style="display:none"><b> <i>“My father died eleven years ago. I was only four then. I never thought I’d hear from him again, but now we’re writing a book together.”</i> </b><br /><br />Sometimes, there's a disarming beauty in simplicity. Sometimes, describing ordinary feelings and doubts is enough for writing extraordinary books. This is the case. I'm so happy that this is my first Gaarder's book. I love how he evokes emotions in the reader's mind without even trying to manipulate them. <br /><br />The plot isn't quite about philosophy, but it also isn't about the romance. It's about whether or not life is worth living without any regrets and if it is, how to be happy with the people you care about. It's also about growing up and fighting for what we want. Gaarder had done a beautiful job in not making this a self-help book. <br /><br />You may think this is some supernatural novel or that Georg is insane, but it's none of the above. Georg is a curious 15-year-old kid whose biggest problem is how to talk to a girl he likes. Then he finds a letter from his father. A letter, his dad wrote to him a few weeks before he died. In that envelope was everything he's father ever wanted to talk to him about but he couldn't because Georg was too young. Mainly, Jan Olav writes about the Orange girl.<br /><br /><i>“I've nothing against eye make-up and lipstick. But the fact is that we’re actually living on a planet in space. For me that’s an extraordinary thought. It’s mind-boggling just to think about the existence of space at all. But there are girls who can’t see the universe for eye liner.” </i><br /><br />The story is more about Jan Ulav and his perspective on life. The reader also has an insight into Georg's head and they both entwine. They're so much alike. Georg is mature for his age, but still is just a teenager, and there are some funny moments where it's clearly shown. Like, when he didn't want to go jogging with his stepfather because he said the running will help him get rid of acne and Georg didn't want to let him know that acne bothers him. <br /><br /><i>“The reason I cried wasn’t just because I knew I might soon be leaving you. I cried because you were so young. I cried because the two of us couldn’t have a proper talk.”</i><br /><br />It's merely a start of the book and I found myself in tears. Jan Olav wasn't only sad because he's dying, he was sad because he'll never truly know his son. He spent only three years with him and couldn't get enough of him and suddenly, his happiness is cruelly taken away. The reason for it is not war, divorce nor natural catastrophe, it's actually a disease. I was able to feel Jan Olav's pain like it was mine. I just don't know if it was right for Georg to read the letter his father left for him. <br /><br /><i> “The truth is that I feel totally helpless, or totally inconsolable, to be more honest. I’m not trying to hide it, but it’s something you’re not to worry about.” </i><br /><br />One of the worst feelings in the world is when you're helpless. And yet, Jan Olav had to live day after day with that feeling and tried to be happy in front of his son, because he was too young to comprehend what was going on. He tried to cherish the time they had left, but he couldn't help the sorrow. Honestly, Gaarder does a painfully good job at describing all those emotions. <br /><br /><i>“I noticed nothing that Sunday except that the Orange Girl was missing. I only had eyes for what wasn’t there.”</i><br /><br />At first, I thought Jan Olav's search for the Orange girl was obsessive and unhealthy. He let his "hunt" get in a way of him living his life. He wanted her to be a part of his world and he was so persistent, nothing could change his mind about it. But then again, he simply KNEW she was the one and he didn't want to let her get away. <br /><br /><i>“<i>I</i> can wait until my heart bleeds with sorrow.” </i><br /><br />Usually, I would find this quote pathetic or sappy, but in this case, I find it rather poetic. This is simply a part of Jan Olav's charm. In his viewpoint, he met his soul mate and he doesn't want to wait. Said on a right occasion and by a right man, this could melt any girl's heart. Most of us just don't want to admit it. <br /><br /><i>“I'm not just some butterfly for you to catch.” </i> <br /><br />The Orange girl is no cookie. She's independent, unafraid of exploring her affinities. Without a doubt, she isn't reluctant to getting what she wants, but she also doesn't let her inconsistent desires get in a way. She wants Jan Olav the same way he wants her and honestly their love story is one of the most beautiful romances I've ever read about. <br /><br /><i>“We can't own each other's past. The question is whether we have a future together.” </i> <br /><br />It should be simple like that. There are no big twists in the story. You can guess at the beginning who the Orange girl is and it isn't a bad thing. The book would be pointless if she was anyone else. I just wish I could find out more about the Orange Girl's reaction after reading the letter. We see only glimpses. <br /><br /><i>“If I’d chosen never to the foot inside the great fairytale, I’d never have known what I’ve lost. Do you see what I’m getting at? Sometimes it’s worse for us human beings to lose something dear to us than never to have had it at all.” </i> <br /><br />It's not about where you are or what you're doing, it's about people you're with. It's about having a circle of your closest ones and always be with them. But what happens when you have to leave them against your will? Is it really better to have nothing? I don't think so. I just love how Gaarder raises existential questions out of tough life choices. <br /><br /><i>“I ask again what would you have chosen if you’d had the chance? Would you have elected to live a short span on earth only to be wrenched away from it all, never ever to return? Or would you have said no, thank you? You have only these two choices. Those are the rules. In choosing to live, you also choose to die.”</i><br /><br />Georg made a beautiful comparison over what Jan Olav said. If there was a choice for him to try the best flavor of his favorite ice cream, but only a milligram, he'd refuse. But, he'd still choose life despite how short it is. For a teenager, George is incredibly insightful and mature, while still managing not to become an adult too early.<br /><br />Overall, it was a poignant read. There were some parts so cute that I was actually hugging the book and other parts so depressing I cried. This is the best kind of book. It's not about the genre, it's about the emotions the book evokes. Occasionally, the book doesn't need to have an astonishing plot to be good, sometimes it's all about the characters like Jan Olav and Georg.<br /><br /><a href="www.brooksofreviews.wordpress.com" rel="nofollow noopener"> Check out my blog! </a></span>
  <a data-text-id="13300017505237209324" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2521788022" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2521788022" />
    <a title="Kenny" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1642452-kenny"><img alt="Kenny" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1472073986p2/1642452.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2521788022?book_show_action=true">Sep 19, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kenny" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kenny" href="/user/show/1642452-kenny">Kenny</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1642452-kenny?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1642452-kenny?shelf=european">european</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1642452-kenny?shelf=sr%C4%91an-says-so">srđan-says-so</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2521788022" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13738673856768176406"><b><i>“But the dream of something unlikely has its own special name. We call it hope.”</i> <br/>― Jostein Gaarder, The Orange Girl</b><br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1537504502i/26331574._SX540_.jpg' alt='1' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/><b>The Orange Girl</b> was my introduction to <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1388082.Jostein_Gaarder' rel='nofollow noopener'>Jostein Gaarder</a>; Thanks to my friend, <b>ST</b> for suggesting <b>Gaarder</b> to me. <b>The Orange Girl</b> was the perfect book to read after finishing <b>Ulysses</b>.<br/> <br/><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1388082.Jostein_Gaarder' rel='nofollow noopener'> Gaarder</a> is a true romantic and <b>The Orange Girl</b> is as fine a <i>feel good</i> book as there is. <br/><br/> <img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1537504502i/26331572._SX540_.jpg' alt='1' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>While reading <b>The Orange Girl</b>, you will experience a wide range of emotions ~~ you will laugh, cry, be frustrated, </span>
  <span id="freeText13738673856768176406" style="display:none"><b><i>“But the dream of something unlikely has its own special name. We call it hope.”</i> <br>― Jostein Gaarder, The Orange Girl</b><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1537504502i/26331574._SX540_.jpg" alt="1" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><b>The Orange Girl</b> was my introduction to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1388082.Jostein_Gaarder" rel="nofollow noopener">Jostein Gaarder</a>; Thanks to my friend, <b>ST</b> for suggesting <b>Gaarder</b> to me. <b>The Orange Girl</b> was the perfect book to read after finishing <b>Ulysses</b>.<br> <br><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1388082.Jostein_Gaarder" rel="nofollow noopener"> Gaarder</a> is a true romantic and <b>The Orange Girl</b> is as fine a <i>feel good</i> book as there is. <br><br> <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1537504502i/26331572._SX540_.jpg" alt="1" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>While reading <b>The Orange Girl</b>, you will experience a wide range of emotions ~~ you will laugh, cry, be frustrated, and think WTF Jan Olav??? This beautiful story will definitely affect you in ways you don't quite expect.<br><br><b>The Orange Girl</b> is short, and handsomely written. At a slim 151 pages, <b>Gaarder</b> will take you on a journey thru love, death, family, and quantum mechanics while making you question everything you thought you believed in, but in wonderful, heartfelt ways. At the heart of all this, is a question our hero, Georg, must answer for his father. It maybe the most important question Georg is ever asked. <br><br>While on this journey of self discovery with his father, Georg learns about his parents courtship, his roots, synchronicty, and the power of love in all her forms. It is an eye opening journey that should make all of <b>Gaarder's</b> readers question who they are, where they come from and most importantly, to ask thoughtful, probing questions about what they perceive as their reality. <br><br>My only real gripe with <b>The Orange Girl</b> is its poor English translation. There were times the translation left me scratching my head wondering why wasn't this proofed better? <br><br>Ultimately, I loved <b>The Orange Girl</b>. It is wonderfully written, and well worth you time.<br><br> <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1537504502i/26331573._SX540_.jpg" alt="1" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>
  <a data-text-id="13738673856768176406" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Nuzhat " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/305943-nuzhat"><img alt="Nuzhat " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1590083834p2/305943.jpg" /></a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nuzhat " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nuzhat " href="/user/show/305943-nuzhat">Nuzhat </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">anyone who wants to learn to let go.</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer5329949" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11641279198708948735">This book helped me be calm and still in a very difficult time in my life. I borrowed it from a friend, Shehneela, when I chanced upon it in her hostel room. I did not have any great reasons to pick this book up when she offered me to select something from her bookshelf, but now I am glad I picked this one up.<br/><br/>I cannot explain in simple words how this book has helped untie a knot in my heart and mind, but it has, and for that reason, I wanted to do away with Shehneela's copy altogether. I was uns</span>
  <span id="freeText11641279198708948735" style="display:none">This book helped me be calm and still in a very difficult time in my life. I borrowed it from a friend, Shehneela, when I chanced upon it in her hostel room. I did not have any great reasons to pick this book up when she offered me to select something from her bookshelf, but now I am glad I picked this one up.<br /><br />I cannot explain in simple words how this book has helped untie a knot in my heart and mind, but it has, and for that reason, I wanted to do away with Shehneela's copy altogether. I was unsuccessful. But the great thing about this book was that it helped me <i>let go</i>. And I did.<br /><br />A year later, I spotted a copy in the bookshop (Staples) beside my new office (Ovex Tech). I did not have the money to buy it at that time, so each day I went to the shop in my lunch break to check if the copy was still there. As soon as I got my first pay cheque cashed, I went in and bought the book.<br /><br />It was a small victory, but a great feeling at the same time. I do not know why anyone would be so attached to a slim book by a not-so-well-known author but I suppose the circumstances hold the secret.<br /><br />All I can say about this book is that it is straightforward, well-written and Gaarder does not repeat himself in this tale, as he has most commonly done in many of his other books. The narrative is crisp, with quick, realistic transitions in the tale, a resolution and well, it's just a good story well told. Something I won't mind reading again and again.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11641279198708948735" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText13255378679233686529" style="display:none">Lately I had been having trouble dealing with existentialism. I even nagged to a dear friend that why don't we get a choice in that...why can't we choose to exist or not to,living in this dark world hurts and I can't take it anymore... and then the universe decided to mock me and put this amazingly written book in my hand in the midst of my visionary thoughts. If you have ever read any of the gaarder's book you're gonna think this must be so much about philosophy...Well it's not, I mean it is. but it's not....You'll get it when you read it . It's a guide on how to live, how to notice the universe around you and let it be observed... and to answer your question(because I owe it to you that much as a reader of the book), maybe if I had been given the chance I'd refuse to take it all and well that would have been such a big shame. But lucky me, I'm alive, I exist and I'm able to notice and enjoy the universe and LIVE the days of my life because who knows maybe the screens never gets reloaded and this is the only chance we get at being mesmerised by our surroundings.</span>

<div id="review_1571004772" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1571004772" />
    <a title="Angela" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4821718-angela"><img alt="Angela" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1393297164p2/4821718.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1571004772?book_show_action=true">Mar 05, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Angela" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Angela" href="/user/show/4821718-angela">Angela</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4821718-angela?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4821718-angela?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1571004772" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3882479641507191262">I once had a really horrible boyfriend (actually, I’ve had several), and while he busied himself with Crown lager and transforming into Mr Hyde I would bury myself in ‘Sophie’s World’, a book lent to me by a kind aunty with impeccable timing. The solace I found among those pages initiated a quest to read more of the Norwegian writer’s stories, much like my later obsession with Paolo Coelho or Juliet Marillier. <br/><br/>But Jostein Gaarder’s offerings are a bit hit and miss. The curse of being a seasoned </span>
  <span id="freeText3882479641507191262" style="display:none">I once had a really horrible boyfriend (actually, I’ve had several), and while he busied himself with Crown lager and transforming into Mr Hyde I would bury myself in ‘Sophie’s World’, a book lent to me by a kind aunty with impeccable timing. The solace I found among those pages initiated a quest to read more of the Norwegian writer’s stories, much like my later obsession with Paolo Coelho or Juliet Marillier. <br /><br />But Jostein Gaarder’s offerings are a bit hit and miss. The curse of being a seasoned authorial fan lies in the comparison of ‘other’ works to the big, wonderful ‘one’ that finds its way onto the shelf in more than one edition. ‘Sophie’s World’, Gaarders most famous YA novel on the history of philosophy, is an epic work that began my love affair with the ideas of ancient and modern thinkers. On this pedestal is also ‘The Solitaire Mystery’, a fantastical adventure story about a son and his father on an expedition with a living, dancing deck of cards. <br /><br />‘The Christmas Mystery’ however was a diluted version of ‘The Solitaire Mystery’ and sits in my mind next to ‘Maya’, an Indonesian journey that entertains the spiritual search for meaning. I’m afraid that ‘The Orange Girl’ also belongs in this second category: Gaarder’s prose is so beautiful, and the lovely turn of phrase never fails to surround me with magic and delight. But the story that pours from a letter written to a teenage boy by his deceased father lacks much in the way of plot or intrigue. <br /><br />The missive from Jan to the narrator was hidden for years in an old pram and now that it’s been found, we sit with Georg as he reads his father’s words and provides for us a commentary about his family, his life, and his interests. Attempting to convey nostalgia, ‘The Orange Girl’ tells the love story of the boy’s parents and the saddening circumstances that pulled Jan from his wife and young son. <br /><br />This is a sweet novella about a man who posthumously desires that his son should know him. It smacks with sentiment, but my reaction was not so much ‘oh, life is so precious!’ but rather ‘this is lovely, but so what?’ (I feel so dirty being harsh to the man who wrote the book that still saves me from Plato’s allegory of the cave.) <br /><br />‘The Orange Girl’ is a character led story, which is all well and good but it needed to have more to drive it forward in order to sustain my attention and distract me from nasty, beer-guzzling bogans. <br /><br />A sentimental 3 stars. <br /><br />Follow me on the blog! ----- &gt; <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://ponderdeeper.wordpress.com/2016/03/06/the-orange-girl-by-jostein-gaarder-review/">https://ponderdeeper.wordpress.com/20...</a> <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3882479641507191262" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<span id="freeTextContainer14297492999785226401">This was a lovely story. 15 year old Georg is handed a letter his now-deceased father wrote to him 12 years ago. Georg was only 3 years old when his father passed away, therefore has no real memories of him. The letter his father writes recounts a story of "the orange girl."<br/><br/>I liked how the story was structured; first Georg reads a section of his father's letter and then he digests and questions what he's just read. As the letter progresses, we can see Georg's thinking process and see how he evol</span>
  <span id="freeText14297492999785226401" style="display:none">This was a lovely story. 15 year old Georg is handed a letter his now-deceased father wrote to him 12 years ago. Georg was only 3 years old when his father passed away, therefore has no real memories of him. The letter his father writes recounts a story of "the orange girl."<br /><br />I liked how the story was structured; first Georg reads a section of his father's letter and then he digests and questions what he's just read. As the letter progresses, we can see Georg's thinking process and see how he evolves. <br /><br />Be warned: I got a bit teary-eyed towards the end of the book.</span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer241008606" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer12302900247277224303">This was one awesome book! I actually just came to read it because of my seat mate way back in my senior year in high school. She was the one who owns the book actually. I saw the book on her desk and asked her if she could give me a gist of the story. She told me that it is better if I read the book myself because she finds it a bit difficult to explain what is in the book. I didn't hesitate, I immediately grabbed that opportunity to be able to read the book. I was so curious about its cover an</span>
  <span id="freeText12302900247277224303" style="display:none">This was one awesome book! I actually just came to read it because of my seat mate way back in my senior year in high school. She was the one who owns the book actually. I saw the book on her desk and asked her if she could give me a gist of the story. She told me that it is better if I read the book myself because she finds it a bit difficult to explain what is in the book. I didn't hesitate, I immediately grabbed that opportunity to be able to read the book. I was so curious about its cover and its title "The Orange Girl". In the process of reading the book, I just found myself so obsessed that I seem can't stop reading. It seems so yummy that whenever I find my free time I always feel the need to have even just a few moments with the book. I found myself so carried away---sniffing and then crying in the process of reading. I understood fully well why it was hard for my classmate to explain the story. When another friend asked about the book, I just told her to read it for herself so she could also feel the satisfaction I myself felt after reading the whole book. <br /><br />Yeah... One thing that struck me... "No two oranges are alike"... and I promise, I will read this book again. ^_^<br /><br />I admire Jostein Gaarder for his way of writing. The story of <i>The Orange Girl</i> is so unique that I can't seem to find the best words to describe it. It's just so wonderfully awesome...<br /><br /><i>Awesome!</i><br /><br /><i>Will read Sophie's World too... Thank you so much Jostein Gaarder!</i> </span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1072093862" />
    <a title="Hajer" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4593570-hajer"><img alt="Hajer" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1515535058p2/4593570.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1072093862?book_show_action=true">May 03, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Hajer" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Hajer" href="/user/show/4593570-hajer">Hajer</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4593570-hajer?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1072093862" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2629890277628760397">I don't know how to start talking about this book. All I know is, after finishing it, I said: Beauty lies in simplicity.<br/>I've read Jostein Gaarder's other book "Sophie's world" many years ago, so I was expecting this one to be a bit complex. And it was not! Still, it was a thought-provoking story, a modern fairytale with a bittersweet ending.<br/>As the whole book is about George reading a letter from his dead father written eleven years ago, we will get to see how his father was telling the story, an</span>
  <span id="freeText2629890277628760397" style="display:none">I don't know how to start talking about this book. All I know is, after finishing it, I said: Beauty lies in simplicity.<br />I've read Jostein Gaarder's other book "Sophie's world" many years ago, so I was expecting this one to be a bit complex. And it was not! Still, it was a thought-provoking story, a modern fairytale with a bittersweet ending.<br />As the whole book is about George reading a letter from his dead father written eleven years ago, we will get to see how his father was telling the story, and what reality looks like.<br />In a way or another, Jostein Gaarder was telling us that after all fairytales are not that beautiful, and fairies are not always as pure as we think they are. And unlike fairytales, this one had a bittersweet ending, at least for those reading the letter (George, and us).<br />I cried many times throughout the book, especially in the end. Maybe the story wasn't that sad, but the whole concept was so touching.<br />It was a light read, a page turner with a touch of existenciality.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2629890277628760397" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_437178060" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/437178060" />
    <a title="♥" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/12416156"><img alt="♥" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1346474967p2/12416156.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/437178060?book_show_action=true">Oct 18, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="♥" class="user" itemprop="url" name="♥" href="/user/show/12416156">♥</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer437178060" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17770155076260643356">Well, it has been many years since I read a book, cover to cover, over the course of a day or two. The Orange Girl has restored my faith in my ability to do just this. I have found that every book I have read of Gaarder's has held me captivated and I love the way in which not one word is wasted nor is it skimmed over (on my part!). This book is no exception.<br/><br/>The Orange Girl's main focus is on Georg, his deceased father and his father's love and obsession with the 'Orange Girl'. Georg's father die</span>
  <span id="freeText17770155076260643356" style="display:none">Well, it has been many years since I read a book, cover to cover, over the course of a day or two. The Orange Girl has restored my faith in my ability to do just this. I have found that every book I have read of Gaarder's has held me captivated and I love the way in which not one word is wasted nor is it skimmed over (on my part!). This book is no exception.<br /><br />The Orange Girl's main focus is on Georg, his deceased father and his father's love and obsession with the 'Orange Girl'. Georg's father died when he was nearly 4 and 11 years later he his handed a large, unopened envelope from his grandmother. The envelope holds his father's story of the Orange Girl. Written just before his death, Georg's father writes to the 'older' Georg about the girl and the events leading up to them meeting. On receiving the envelope, Georg hides himself away for an evening to read the story.<br /><br />Gaarder brings his wonderful use of a 'story within story' in to play. We are with Georg all the way as he reads his father's tale and we also witness Georg's feelings and views throughout the book. We are, in effect, seeing Georg growing up in the course of a few hours. We are able to see him getting to know a father who died long before he was old enough to get to know him in life.<br /><br />It was within the early part of the book I felt I had discovered the 'mystery', however, this did not make the book any less enchanting. In fact it made the story all the more bittersweet, poignant and touching. I found the Orange Girl to be a wonderful book about love, loss, life and making the most of what you have before it has gone. <br /><br />Often with Gaarder's books I find that I stop myself for a few minutes, remove myself from the daily drudgery of work, bed, work, and see life for what it is! Precious, short and not to be spent being too serious about things for any length of time! The Orange Girl made me do this once again, even if for a short time, before the world of work, stress and earning money beckons!</span>
  <a data-text-id="17770155076260643356" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer17732191223684616265">As much as I disliked the way the story was narrated, I must confess the ending was breathtaking! My answer is YES, I would definitely choose to live in this world full of wonders even if it's too short or even if we have to leave it so early. Plus, based on my religious beliefs, we have been asked to be brought to this world before birth! So, I stand by my choice to live this life, which is a gift to those who embrace it.   <span id="freeText17732191223684616265" style="display:none">As much as I disliked the way the story was narrated, I must confess the ending was breathtaking! My answer is YES, I would definitely choose to live in this world full of wonders even if it's too short or even if we have to leave it so early. Plus, based on my religious beliefs, we have been asked to be brought to this world before birth! So, I stand by my choice to live this life, which is a gift to those who embrace it. Just so you know, I'm one of those who enjoy single little things, even having a cup of tea with a biscuit!! A little piece of advice: Read this book if you are on the edge of loosing it, it will be worth it, may rescue you from all the irritating thoughts.</span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nazanin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nazanin" href="/user/show/42037313-nazanin">Nazanin</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer2010781462" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer6436090056685089183">this book had some unique ideas. like how our world is a big fairy tale. and as i was reading i felt a little sorry that we could no longer see the magic. when i look at a tree, I see green leaves that can catch the light and turn it into different chemicals. I think about the mechanism of photosynthesis, and how the vessels in it's trunk transfer different materials to every part of the tree. because i know the rules, a tree doesn't mesmerize me anymore, I don't see the magic when i look at it.</span>
  <span id="freeText6436090056685089183" style="display:none">this book had some unique ideas. like how our world is a big fairy tale. and as i was reading i felt a little sorry that we could no longer see the magic. when i look at a tree, I see green leaves that can catch the light and turn it into different chemicals. I think about the mechanism of photosynthesis, and how the vessels in it's trunk transfer different materials to every part of the tree. because i know the rules, a tree doesn't mesmerize me anymore, I don't see the magic when i look at it. i wonder, would I rather not have all these information, and as Jan Olav says, see the human as a human being and not a combination of organs? I'm not so sure. because maybe that makes the magic even more powerful, the fact that a combination of flesh and bone and cells can do so many impressive things and can think so beautifully, is pretty mesmerizing in itself.<br /><br />and as for the big question, personally i would want to live. because what happens to you in that alternate version where you choose not to be born? you simply don't exist. i think life is not just about "the short span" you have in this world. think about what would happen if Darwin or Einstein had chosen not to be born. this doesn't necessarily mean you should do sth that big for the world to make your existence count, I believe we all do change a lot of things in many lives in the time we get to live here. so would i take the chance to live in a big fairy tale, to love, to be happy, to make changes even though i knew it would one day be over? yes, I certainly would.</span>

<div id="review_1046971675" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1046971675" />
    <a title="Julie Mestdagh" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/27315109-julie-mestdagh"><img alt="Julie Mestdagh" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1388820262p2/27315109.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1046971675?book_show_action=true">Sep 06, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Julie Mestdagh" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Julie Mestdagh" href="/user/show/27315109-julie-mestdagh">Julie Mestdagh</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1046971675" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4677509261177749893">"No two oranges are alike…."<br/><br/>Amazing. Breathtaking. Emotional. After having read this book in one stretch in one day, I find myself quite content in the sofa, my thoughts wandering all over the place, feeling both lucky, happy, sad and nostalgic at the same time. What a book! <br/><br/>"The orange girl" by Jostein Gaarder is actually a story written by two authors. When Georg, a Norwegian boy whose favourite words are "yes please, a bit more of both" , is 15 years old, his grandparents show up with a lette</span>
  <span id="freeText4677509261177749893" style="display:none">"No two oranges are alike…."<br /><br />Amazing. Breathtaking. Emotional. After having read this book in one stretch in one day, I find myself quite content in the sofa, my thoughts wandering all over the place, feeling both lucky, happy, sad and nostalgic at the same time. What a book! <br /><br />"The orange girl" by Jostein Gaarder is actually a story written by two authors. When Georg, a Norwegian boy whose favourite words are "yes please, a bit more of both" , is 15 years old, his grandparents show up with a letter they found, addressed to him. Nothing peculiar, were it not that the letter was written by Georg's father, who passed away when Georg was 3 years old. In this letter, Georg is told an important story and asked an important question by his father. Georg, in return, replies after every couple of paragraphs, sharing his thoughts and memories with the readers, making the book some kind of duet divided over time.<br /><br />"My father died eleven years ago. I was only four then. I never thought I'd hear from him again, but now we're writing a book together".<br /><br />The story Georg's father writes about is one about the orange girl. I can't go into detail about that without spoiling the book, so I won't. What I can say is that it was brilliant how Georg's reactions to his father's story evolve throughout the book. How a 15 year old boy responds to some difficult life questions ("why are we here and is it worth living for" and the concept of "if my parents hadn't met by coincidence, I would have never existed") by relating these to his day-to-day life. Georg's emotions while ploughing through his father's message (curiousness, surprise, sadness, anger, recognition,…) are very well developed and very touching. It's also very touching to compare the father's side of some father-son moments and the fragments Georg remembers. <br /><br />"sometimes it is worse for us humans to lose something dear to us than to have never had it at all"<br /><br />"Can I be certain that there is no life after this one? ….. I remember how, a couple of days ago, you and I killed a couple of hours with a computer game. Each time we died in that game, a new screen immediately came up and we were off again. How can we know that there isn't a new screen for our souls as well?"<br /><br />(on love) "We used the "we" pronoun more often and often….When we use the "we" pronoun we place two people behind a joint action almost as if they form a composite being. This pronoun is called dual, or something that is shared by two - and only two - people. I think it's a useful concept because there are times when subjects are neither single nor many. You are 'we two', and a 'we two' as if that 'we' cannot be split". … "We'll make dinner". "We'll open a bottle of wine". "We'll go to bed". Talking like that is almost shameless, isn't it? At any rate, it's quite different to saying: you must take the bus home now because I want to get some sleep…."<br /><br />The setup of the book is excellent. Things that seem strange or coincidental at the beginning, fall into place throughout the story. For example his father asking in the beginning of the letter how things would be with the Hubble Space Telescope by the time Georg reads the letter, which is just weeks after Georg did a special assignment on just that subject just weeks before. Later we learn that Georg's father sat and talked to Georg about stars at night.<br /><br />I loved reading it from beginning to end, and it left me with a lot of food for thought. <br />A great story on life, love, father-son relationships and lots more! <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4677509261177749893" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lynossa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lynossa" href="/user/show/4499903-lynossa">Lynossa</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer130540418" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12849389629055804396">Few days ago Kay-c asked me what book should she read if she wants to start reading philosophy book. I immediately said' Jostein Gaarder's!'. Jostein Gaarder was the reason why I read philosophy book in the first place. I read Sophie's World years ago and I was hooked! So I started reading his other books. Orange Girl isn't my favorite Gaarder but that doesn't mean this book isn't worthy of your time. On the contrary, I think this is one of the must-read book; especially if you're taking interes</span>
  <span id="freeText12849389629055804396" style="display:none">Few days ago Kay-c asked me what book should she read if she wants to start reading philosophy book. I immediately said' Jostein Gaarder's!'. Jostein Gaarder was the reason why I read philosophy book in the first place. I read Sophie's World years ago and I was hooked! So I started reading his other books. Orange Girl isn't my favorite Gaarder but that doesn't mean this book isn't worthy of your time. On the contrary, I think this is one of the must-read book; especially if you're taking interesting in philosophy. In my opinion every book that made you thought about the meaning of your life was in some way engaging you in philosophical thinking; and this book is one of those books. However unlike Sophie's World, philosophy in this book was presented subtly and you barely realize it. That's why I consider this as a very good start if you want to read philosophy novels.<br /><br /><br />The character, Georg, was cute and very smart. Most of Gaarder's books feature kids as his main character and he's good at creating believable characters. The plot was a bit slow in the beginning but as you progress in reading you will discover many interesting things; beautiful things and sad things. I was completely blown away by the ending, it was so sad but it make sense to me, it kinda complete my imagination of Georg's father and it didn't disappoint me at all. I think this story will capture the heart of everyone who experienced the lost of a beloved person.</span>

<div id="review_637090569" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/637090569" />
    <a title="Bjørn Zeiler" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/21157567-bj-rn-zeiler"><img alt="Bjørn Zeiler" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1457993751p2/21157567.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/637090569?book_show_action=true">Jun 09, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bjørn Zeiler" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bjørn Zeiler" href="/user/show/21157567-bj-rn-zeiler">Bjørn Zeiler</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Everyone</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Bjørn by: </span>
      Helene Petersen
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21157567-bj-rn-zeiler?shelf=depth">depth</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer637090569" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14204649021299812346">A friend of mine told me that this was her favorite book and I am happy to say that I can definitely see why.<br/> It is a wonderfully written story combining the presence and wonder of an essay with the tension and romance of a novel.<br/><br/>It leaves you with a sort of childish wonder which you suddenly notice that you've missed. The world is so "figured out" with science and politics that we sometimes forget what it feels like to look upon it and simply "wonder".<br/> The book explains this and manages to pull</span>
  <span id="freeText14204649021299812346" style="display:none">A friend of mine told me that this was her favorite book and I am happy to say that I can definitely see why.<br /> It is a wonderfully written story combining the presence and wonder of an essay with the tension and romance of a novel.<br /><br />It leaves you with a sort of childish wonder which you suddenly notice that you've missed. The world is so "figured out" with science and politics that we sometimes forget what it feels like to look upon it and simply "wonder".<br /> The book explains this and manages to pull you out of your every-day routine and relieve you of some of your hard-earned knowledge, so that you may once again wonder just how fast a bumblebee really is, or how magical the stars appear.<br /><br />I can imagine this could be done in a very un-appealing way, but by letting the young narrator discover all these concepts himself in an essay makes it seem so simple and manages to convey that the narrator isn't trying to sound overly smart or clever; he simply wonders.<br /><br />One of the best books I've read so far in the genre, a definite equal to classics like "The Little Prince" and "The Alchemist". </span>
  <a data-text-id="14204649021299812346" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_545789827" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/545789827" />
    <a title="Kerli" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17534959-kerli"><img alt="Kerli" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1361358908p2/17534959.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/545789827?book_show_action=true">Feb 25, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kerli" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kerli" href="/user/show/17534959-kerli">Kerli</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">everyone. READ IT!!</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Kerli by: </span>
      Sophie's World
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17534959-kerli?shelf=life-changers">life-changers</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17534959-kerli?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17534959-kerli?shelf=beautiful-books">beautiful-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17534959-kerli?shelf=interesting">interesting</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17534959-kerli?shelf=girly-stuff">girly-stuff</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17534959-kerli?shelf=mind-food">mind-food</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17534959-kerli?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer545789827" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6736849950553978165">What a beautiful, moving book. I read Sophie's World, because someone recommended it to me (I think it might have been just a random man in the library), and I wasn't that impressed, but his imaginative world was enough for me to try and read more - so I got this and Solitaire Mystery out as well. And I loved both of them.<br/><br/>I can't really say why I liked this one so much. I am not a chick-lit fan, the only romances I have actually read and liked are by Austen, so this was not as much about the rel</span>
  <span id="freeText6736849950553978165" style="display:none">What a beautiful, moving book. I read Sophie's World, because someone recommended it to me (I think it might have been just a random man in the library), and I wasn't that impressed, but his imaginative world was enough for me to try and read more - so I got this and Solitaire Mystery out as well. And I loved both of them.<br /><br />I can't really say why I liked this one so much. I am not a chick-lit fan, the only romances I have actually read and liked are by Austen, so this was not as much about the relationship between the father and the elusive Orange Girl, but about characters generally. We don't tend to think that people that have died were real people with their own wishes, dreams, emotions etc, just as we don't often regard our parents (or teachers!) as people with emotions as acute as ours. I must say here that I have been an empathic person since I was very young and did not hate my teachers and always tried to be nice to people, but only now, as an adult, and a parent myself have I started being more interested in my parents' and grandparents' minds and characters. (I guess as kids we are too preoccupied trying to figure out our own feelings about stuff and how to use them.)<br /><br />So we come to Georg, who has never really known his father, and then, one day he receives his father's diary/letters, written to him years ago, when he knew he was dying and couldn't be there for Georg. What follows is a beautiful story about how his father met his mother and realization that everyone is a person. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[And I loved the part where Georg realized he actually remembered his dad showing him the stars one night he was three and that is why he has been interested in astronomy all his life. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> There are also a few other surprises hidden in there, but you'll have to find out for yourself.<br /><br />It was incredibly moving when I first read it and I am crying even now as I write this review. Whether it is because I understand the despair of a parent who knows he can't be there for his child as he grows up, or just because I felt sad that someone who "had it all" had to die, I can't tell at this moment. But it is a wonderful book and I would recommend it to everyone.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6736849950553978165" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_761654389" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/761654389" />
    <a title="Kareem A.F." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5140541-kareem-a-f"><img alt="Kareem A.F." src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/761654389?book_show_action=true">Nov 10, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kareem A.F." class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kareem A.F." href="/user/show/5140541-kareem-a-f">Kareem A.F.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer761654389" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13880697051745665287">Simple but deep - that's my opinion of "The Orange Girl". At the beginning of the story, the main character - Georg - does not really now a lot about his deceased father who died when Georg was at a very young age (4 years old). However, matters change when he discovers a letter from his father. Out of curiosity and interest, Georg decides to read it ,thus forming the story. <br/>What's even more remarkable is that the father addressed this letter to his son, knowing he how he would die someday. For,</span>
  <span id="freeText13880697051745665287" style="display:none">Simple but deep - that's my opinion of "The Orange Girl". At the beginning of the story, the main character - Georg - does not really now a lot about his deceased father who died when Georg was at a very young age (4 years old). However, matters change when he discovers a letter from his father. Out of curiosity and interest, Georg decides to read it ,thus forming the story. <br />What's even more remarkable is that the father addressed this letter to his son, knowing he how he would die someday. For, the letter revolves around "The Orange Girl" (her identity remains unknown) whom the father met while on a train and instantly fell in love with. The two are then separated, however, the father attempts to find her again many times and succeeds creating a strong friendship between the both. <br />What really interested me in the story is that it is written in two points of view; Georg's POV as well as his father's in the letter. For, by this, the author creates a smooth and majestic feeling while reading the story. Moreover, it shows how life can be appreciated and enjoyed in unique simple ways. Last of all, I'd like to share my favorite part of the book - the end - which truly teaches this moral of life: <br />"Imagine that you were on the threshold of this fairytale, sometime billions of years ago when everything was created. And you were able to choose whether you wanted to be born to a life on this planet at some point. You wouldn't know when you were going to be born, nor how long you'd live for, but at any event it wouldn't be more than a few years. All you'd know was that, if you chose to come into the world at some point, you'd also have to leave it again one day and go away from everything. What would you have chosen if you'd had the chance? Would you have elected to live a short span on earth only to be wrenched away from it all, never ever to return? Or would you have said no, thank you?”</span>
  <a data-text-id="13880697051745665287" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('bAlXLA8PSIJRIjrv+DPibjTpXZ7gW+rSPvYK9lLRuQ1WLz7+tFGB6vYJjo4Pxuix4ra6Vi7WZLVliExHn5JLlw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('SqeEnRz+kajPgmFkn64xNRF7x/3Y1YhWEUYv+Lbg/19wge1Pp6BYwGip1QVoWzvqxyQgNRZYBjFKOGlJe6MNxQ==')})
  }

It is the tragic monologue of a woman speaking with the child she carries in her womb. This letter confronts the burning theme of abortion, and the meaning of life, by asking difficult questions: Is it fair to impose life even if it means suffering? Would it be better not to be born at all?\nLetter to a Child Never Born touches on the real meaning of being a woman: the power to give life or not. When the book begins, the protagonist is upset after learning she is pregnant. She knows nothing about the child, except that this creature depends totally and uniquely on her own choices. The creation of another person directly within one’s own body is a very shocking thing. The sense of responsibility is huge; it is a heavy burden that gives life to endless reflections, from the origin of our existence to the shame of our selfishness. If the child could choose, would he prefer to be born, to grow up, and to suffer, or would he return to the joyful limbo from which he came? A woman’s freedom and individuality are also challenged by a newborn—should she renounce her freedom, her job, and her choice?       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/1388082.Jostein_Gaarder">About Jostein Gaarder</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/1388082.Jostein_Gaarder"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1546539876p3/1388082.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/1388082.Jostein_Gaarder">Jostein Gaarder
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
5,837
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer8928117555776559485">Jostein Gaarder is a Norwegian intellectual and author of several novels, short stories, and children's books. Gaarder often writes from the perspective of children, exploring their sense of wonder about the world. He often uses meta-fiction in his works, writing stories within stories.<br/><br/>Gaarder was born into a pedagogical family. His best known work is the novel <i>Sophie's World</i>, subtitled "A Novel a</span>
  <span id="freeText8928117555776559485" style="display:none">Jostein Gaarder is a Norwegian intellectual and author of several novels, short stories, and children's books. Gaarder often writes from the perspective of children, exploring their sense of wonder about the world. He often uses meta-fiction in his works, writing stories within stories.<br /><br />Gaarder was born into a pedagogical family. His best known work is the novel <i>Sophie's World</i>, subtitled "A Novel about the History of Philosophy." This popular work has been translated into fifty-three languages; there are over thirty million copies in print, with three million copies sold in Germany alone.<br /><br />In 1997, he established the Sophie Prize together with his wife Siri Dannevig. This prize is an international environment and development prize (USD 100,000 = 77,000 €), awarded annually. It is named after the novel.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8928117555776559485" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/1015565-appelsinpiken">Quotes from The Orange Girl</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;Imagine that you were on the threshold of this fairytale, sometime billions of years ago when everything was created. And you were able to choose whether you wanted to be born to a life on this planet at some point. You wouldn’t know when you were going to be born, nor how long you’d live for, but at any event it wouldn’t be more than a few years. All you’d know was that, if you chose to come into the world at some point, you’d also have to leave it again one day and go away from everything. This might cause you a good deal of grief, as lots of people think that life in the great fairytale is so wonderful that the mere thought of it ending can bring tears to their eyes. Things can be so nice here that it’s terribly painful to think that at some point the days will run out. What would you have chosen, if there had been some higher power that had gave you the choice? Perhaps we can imagine some sort of cosmic fairy in this great, strange fairytale. What you have chosen to live a life on earth at some point, whether short or long, in a hundred thousand or a hundred million years? Or would you have refused to join in the game because you didn’t like the rules? (...) I asked myself the same question maybe times during the past few weeks. Would I have elected to live a life on earth in the firm knowledge that I’d suddenly be torn away from it, and perhaps in the middle of intoxicating happiness? (...) Well, I wasn’t sure what I would have chosen. (...) If I’d chosen never to the foot inside the great fairytale, I’d never have known what I’ve lost. Do you see what I’m getting at? Sometimes it’s worse for us human beings to lose something dear to us than never to have had it at all.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/212867-imagine-that-you-were-on-the-threshold-of-this-fairytale">162 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;If I’d chosen never to the foot inside the great fairytale, I’d never have known what I’ve lost. Do you see what I’m getting at? Sometimes it’s worse for us human beings to lose something dear to us than never to have had it at all.&rdquo;</span>
